reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F
import math


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Triangular self-attention over pair representations, as used in
    AlphaFold2's Evoformer stack.  Starting-node variant: for each
    row i of the pair matrix, standard multi-head self-attention is
    performed across the column dimension j, with an additive pair
    bias.  This lets positions (i,j) and (i,k) exchange information
    through their shared starting node i.
    """

    def __init__(self, pair_dim, num_heads, dropout=0.0):
        super().__init__()
        self.num_heads = num_heads
        self.head_dim = pair_dim // num_heads
        assert pair_dim % num_heads == 0

        self.layer_norm = nn.LayerNorm(pair_dim)
        self.query = nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim, bias=False)
        self.key = nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim, bias=False)
        self.value = nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim, bias=False)
        self.gate = nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim)
        self.bias_proj = nn.Linear(pair_dim, num_heads, bias=False)
        self.out_proj = nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim)
        self.dropout = nn.Dropout(dropout)

    def forward(self, pair_repr: torch.Tensor, pair_mask: torch.Tensor) -> torch.Tensor:
        """
        Args:
            pair_repr: (B, N, N, pair_dim)
            pair_mask: (B, N, N)
        Returns:
            updated pair representation (B, N, N, pair_dim)
        """
        B, N, _, D = pair_repr.shape
        h = self.num_heads
        d = self.head_dim

        x = self.layer_norm(pair_repr)
        g = torch.sigmoid(self.gate(x))

        # Pair bias from the pair representation itself
        bias = self.bias_proj(x).permute(0, 3, 1, 2)  # (B, h, N, N)

        # Reshape to (B*N, N, D) — attention across columns for each row
        x_flat = x.reshape(B * N, N, D)
        q = self.query(x_flat).view(B * N, N, h, d).transpose(1, 2)  # (B*N, h, N, d)
        k = self.key(x_flat).view(B * N, N, h, d).transpose(1, 2)
        v = self.value(x_flat).view(B * N, N, h, d).transpose(1, 2)

        attn = (q @ k.transpose(-1, -2)) / math.sqrt(d)  # (B*N, h, N, N)
        attn = attn.view(B, N, h, N, N)

        # Add pair bias: bias (B, h, N, N) → (B, 1, h, N, N) broadcast across rows
        attn = attn + bias.unsqueeze(1)

        # Apply mask: pair_mask (B, N, N) → mask over the key dimension
        mask = pair_mask.unsqueeze(1).unsqueeze(2)  # (B, 1, 1, N, N) – last dim is keys
        attn = attn.masked_fill(mask == 0, float("-inf"))
        attn = attn.view(B * N, h, N, N)
        attn = F.softmax(attn, dim=-1)
        attn = self.dropout(attn)

        out = (attn @ v).transpose(1, 2).reshape(B, N, N, D)
        out = g * out
        return self.out_proj(out)


pair_dim = 64
num_heads = 4
seq_len = 32
batch_size = 2


def get_inputs():
    pair_repr = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, seq_len, pair_dim)
    pair_mask = torch.ones(batch_size, seq_len, seq_len)
    return [pair_repr, pair_mask]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [pair_dim, num_heads]

reasoning
We need to write a custom CUDA kernel that replaces the forward computation of the provided PyTorch model. The model is essentially a triangular self-attention over pair representations (starting-node variant). It includes: - LayerNorm on pair_repr (B, N, N, D) - gate = sigmoid(Linear_with_bias(pair_repr)) -> (B, N, N, D) - bias_proj = Linear_no_bias(pair_repr) -> (B, N, N, h), then permute to (B, h, N, N) - Flatten x (post-layernorm) to (B*N, N, D) - query = Linear_no_bias(x_flat) -> reshape to (B*N, h, N, d) - key = Linear_no_bias(x_flat) -> reshape - value = Linear_no_bias(x_flat) -> reshape - attn = (q @ k^T) / sqrt(d) -> (B*N, h, N, N) - reshape attn to (B, N, h, N, N) - add bias.unsqueeze(1) -> (B, 1, h, N, N) broadcast - apply mask: pair_mask unsqueeze to (B,1,1,N,N); masked_fill zeros -> -inf - reshape back to (B*N, h, N, N) - softmax over dim=-1 - dropout - out = attn @ v -> transpose and reshape to (B, N, N, D) - out = g * out - out = Linear_with_bias(out) -> (B, N, N, D) We need to write a CUDA kernel that does this faster than PyTorch reference. We need to consider the sizes: pair_dim=64, num_heads=4, head_dim=16, seq_len=32, batch_size=2. So B=2, N=32, D=64, h=4, d=16. This is relatively small. The reference uses standard PyTorch operations, which are well-optimized. However, there is overhead from many operations, reshape, permute, etc. A fused kernel that does everything from the linear projections to the final output projection in one go could be faster, especially for small sizes. But writing a fully fused kernel for all steps is complex. Let's analyze the bottlenecks and see what can be fused. The main steps: 1. LayerNorm 2. Three linear projections (Q, K, V) without bias 3. Gate linear with bias + sigmoid 4. Bias projection linear without bias 5. Compute attention scores Q*K^T / sqrt(d), add bias, mask, softmax, dropout 6. Multiply by V 7. Multiply by gate g 8. Output projection linear with bias Since D=64, h=4, d=16, N=32, B=2, total elements are small. But the reference might still be fast due to cuBLAS for matmuls. However, many small matmuls have overhead. A fused kernel could be beneficial. We need to check GPU specs to know what we're working with. Let's call get_gpu_specs.

reasoning
Let me analyze this reference implementation. This is a triangular self-attention over pair representations, specifically the "starting-node" variant from AlphaFold2's Evoformer. The key operations are: 1. LayerNorm on pair_repr 2. Gate (linear + sigmoid) 3. Bias projection 4. Q, K, V projections 5. Attention computation with bias and mask 6. Softmax, dropout 7. Output projection The dimensions are: - pair_repr: (B, N, N, D) - B=2, N=32, D=64, h=4, d=16 Let me think about how to fuse this into efficient CUDA kernels. The reference does several reshape operations: - x.reshape(B*N, N, D) for QKV - Then attention is computed as (B*N, h, N, N) - Then it's reshaped to (B, N, h, N, N) for bias addition - Then masked, reshaped back to (B*N, h, N, N) for softmax This is a relatively small problem (B=2, N=32), so overhead matters a lot. A fused kernel approach would be best. Let me design the fused kernel strategy: 1. First, we can do a fused kernel that computes: - LayerNorm - Q, K, V projections - Bias projection - Gate 2. Then a second fused kernel for attention: - Q @ K^T / sqrt(d) - Add bias - Add mask - Softmax - @ V 3. Then output projection and gate multiplication Actually, given the small sizes (N=32), we can fuse even more aggressively. N=32 means we can load entire rows/columns into shared memory. For attention: N=32, d=16, h=4 - For each query position i (across B*N rows), we compute attention over 32 keys - Q, K, V shapes per head: (B*N, h, N, d) = (64, 4, 32, 16) - Attention scores per head: (64, 4, 32, 32) Since N=32 is small and d=16 is small, we can try to fuse the entire attention forward pass. Wait, I notice this is the starting-node variant. Looking more carefully: - x_flat = x.reshape(B*N, N, D) where B*N = 64 - q = (B*N, h, N, d) = (64, 4, 32, 16) - attn = q @ k^T -> (64, 4, 32, 32) Then attn is reshaped to (B, N, h, N, N) = (2, 32, 4, 32, 32) And bias is (B, h, N, N) = (2, 4, 32, 32), unsqueezed to (2, 1, h, N, N) So for each batch b, each row i, each head h, we have N=32 attention scores over keys j. The bias is shared across rows i (since bias.unsqueeze(1) broadcasts over the row dimension), meaning for a given head and key position, the bias is the same regardless of which row we're computing. Wait, bias shape is (B, h, N, N). Let me re-read: bias = self.bias_proj(x).permute(0, 3, 1, 2) # (B, h, N, N) Then bias.unsqueeze(1) makes it (B, 1, h, N, N). This broadcasts across the row dimension (dim 1 of the 5D tensor). But in the 5D attn tensor (B, N, h, N, N), dim 1 corresponds to the query row i. So the bias is the same for all query positions in a given batch/head. Actually wait, let me re-check. The attn after q@k^T and reshape is (B, N, h, N, N). Let's call it (B, I, h, J, K) where I is query row, J is query col, K is key col. After bias.unsqueeze(1): (B, 1, h, N, N). So for any (B, I, h, J, K), the bias is just bias[B, h, J, K]. Wait no, the original bias is (B, h, N, N) where the last two dims likely correspond to (query_position, key_position) or something? Wait, x is (B, N, N, D), so bias_proj(x) is (B, N, N, h), and permute(0, 3, 1, 2) gives (B, h, N, N). So the last two N dims are the spatial dimensions. In attention: q @ k^T for a row batch gives shape (N_query_positions, N_key_positions) for that row. When reshaped: (B, N_rows, h, N_query_cols, N_key_cols). Hmm, actually for each of the B*N rows, we have (h, N_query_positions, N_key_positions). Since there are B*N rows total and each has N query positions (for that row batch), the total is B*N*N = B*N^2. Wait, I think there's a subtlety. Let me re-trace: - x_flat = x.reshape(B*N, N, D) -- (B*N rows, each with N positions, D features) - q = (B*N, h, N, d) -- for each of B*N rows, h heads, N query positions, d dims - k = same for keys - v = same for values - q @ k^T = (B*N, h, N, N) -- for each row, each head, N query positions attend to N key positions - attn.view(B, N, h, N, N) -- (B, N_rows, h, N_queries, N_keys) The B*N "rows" in x_flat correspond to flattening (B, N) where each batch has N spatial positions. So for each batch b and row i, we have a self-attention across the N positions of that row. The bias is (B, h, N, N) -- shared across the row dimension when broadcast. So for a given batch b and head h, the bias depends only on the spatial positions (m, n) that are the last two dims. But in the attention tensor (B, N, h, N, N), the last two dims are also spatial. And bias (B, 1, h, N, N) broadcasts over dim 1. So yes, the bias is the same for all N rows in dim 1. Actually wait - I need to check if the last two dims of bias match up with the last two dims of attn. Or is bias (B, h, J, K) where J and K are the spatial dims? And attn has dims (B, I, h, J, K)? Yes, that seems right. So adding bias.unsqueeze(1) adds the same bias matrix to each row I. This makes sense in the context of AlphaFold's triangular attention: the bias is computed from the pair representation. Now, let me think about how to fuse this optimally. Given the small problem size, I think we should write: 1. A fused kernel for computing all linear projections, layernorm, and gate 2. A fused attention kernel that computes QK^T, adds bias, applies mask, softmax, and multiplies by V For N=32, each row of the attention matrix is just 32 elements. We can compute each attention row in a thread block. Let me plan the kernel structure more carefully. Kernel 1: Fused preprocessing Input: pair_repr (B, N, N, D), pair_mask (B, N, N) Output: Q (B*N, h, N, d), K (B*N, h, N, d), V (B*N, h, N, d), bias (B, h, N, N), gate (B, N, N, D) Wait, we don't actually need to output QKV separately if we fuse everything. But for clarity and to save memory, maybe: Actually for H100, we should try to be very aggressive with fusion. Since N=32 is tiny, we can potentially do: Kernel 1: For each (B, N, N, D) element, compute layernorm, then all projections. This would require loading the entire (N, D) row for layer norm. We can have each thread block handle one (B, N) row and compute layer norm over the N*D elements of that row's... wait, layer norm is over the D dimension, not N. LayerNorm: for each position (b, i, j), normalize over D=64 features. Actually, layer norm is along the last dimension (pair_dim). So for each (b, i, j), we compute mean and std over D=64 elements. Then all linear operations (query, key, value, gate, bias_proj) are also applied per position. Strategy: - Each warp or thread block handles a chunk of positions - For each position, we need to: 1. Compute layer norm (load D=64 elements, compute mean/var, normalize) 2. Apply 5 linear projections to get q, k, v, g, bias 3. Store results Since D=64 and we have 5 output projections with total output size D + D + D + D + h = 64*4 + 4 = 260 per position. For h heads and d head_dim: q, k, v each are D, gate is D, bias_proj is h. Total 4D + h = 260 floats per position. Total positions: B * N * N = 2 * 32 * 32 = 2048. If each thread handles one position: 2048 threads total. On H100 that's tiny. We should have each thread process multiple positions or each warp handle a group. Actually for maximum performance on small problems, we want to saturate the SMs. With 2048 positions and each position needing to compute 5 linear projections (each is a matrix-vector multiply of size (out_dim, D)), we can parallelize very well. For the attention kernel: For each of B*N = 64 "row heads" (actually B*N positions in the flattened row dimension) and each of h=4 heads, we compute attention over N=32 keys for N=32 queries. Total attention computations: B*N*h = 64*4 = 256 attention heads. Each attention head has N queries x N keys = 32x32 = 1024 score computations. Each query then has a 32-element softmax and a 32xd matrix multiply. We could have each thread block handle one (row_batch, head) pair, computing all N=32 queries. That's 256 thread blocks total. Actually, let me re-think the dimensions. For x_flat, we have (B*N, N, D) = (64, 32, 64). The Q, K, V for this are (64, h, N, d). For each of the 64 rows and 4 heads, we have Q, K, V each of shape (32, 16). The attention for each (row, head) is: Q @ K^T (32, 32) -> add bias -> add mask -> softmax -> @ V (32, 16). So there are 64 * 4 = 256 such independent attention computations. For each one, N=32 is very small. We could compute all 32 queries in parallel within a thread block. Let me write kernels. For Kernel 1 (preprocessing): I'll have each thread block handle a number of positions. Each thread in the block processes one or more (B, N, N) positions. Kernel 2 (attention): For better efficiency, we can have each thread block handle one (B*N, h) pair, i.e., one attention head. Within the block, we can use shared memory for K and V, and compute QK^T and attention@V. With N=32 and d=16: - Load K (32, 16) to shared memory: 512 floats = 2KB - Load V (32, 16) to shared memory: 2KB - Total shared memory per block: ~4KB + some for Q rows - Block size: 32 threads (one per query) or 256 threads to have more parallelism per query Actually, for a 32x32 attention matrix where each element is a dot product of two 16-dim vectors, we might want more threads. Alternative: use a warp per query. 32 warps = 1024 threads per block would handle all 32 queries. But occupancy might be low with 1024 threads/block. Or: each thread handles one query. 32 threads per block. For each query, the thread computes 32 dot products of 16 elements each (QK^T row), then softmax over 32 elements, then the output is the weighted sum of 32 value vectors of 16 elements. 32 threads per block means very low occupancy but we have 256 blocks, so that might be okay on H100. Or: each block handles multiple attention heads. Say 8 heads per block -> 256 threads = 8 warps. Each warp handles one head? That could work. Actually, the simplest approach that avoids complex shared memory coalescing issues: for each attention head, use a block of 256 threads (or 128) where threads cooperate to load K and V to shared memory, and then each thread is assigned some queries. Wait, let me think about what's simplest and fastest. With N=32 and d=16: - The attention score matrix is 32x32 - Each score is a dot product of 16 elements If we use 32 threads in a block, each thread handles one row (query). To compute the 32 scores for that row, each thread does 32 dot products of 16 elements = 512 multiply-adds. Then softmax over 32 elements (single thread). Then the output is a weighted combination of 32 vectors of 16 elements = 512 multiply-adds. Total ~1024 FMAs per thread. With 256 blocks (one per head), total work is 256 * 32 * 1024 ≈ 8.4M FMAs. This is tiny for H100. The limiting factor is memory bandwidth and kernel launch overhead. So we want to minimize memory movement by aggressive fusion. Let me write a heavily fused kernel. Since N=32 is small, we might even be able to keep Q, K, V in registers or shared memory for the entire attention computation. Here's a plan for a single giant fused kernel: For each (b, i, j) position: 1. Load pair_repr[b, i, :, :] for the entire row i? No, layer norm is per position. Actually, for the attention part, the Q, K, V projections use x_flat = x.reshape(B*N, N, D). This means for a given (b, i) flattened row, Q, K, V depend only on x[b, i, :, :] (the N positions in that row). For bias_proj, it depends on all x[b, :, :, :]. So we'd need to load the entire (B, N, N, D) tensor. That's fine. Let me write two kernels: 1. Kernel that computes layer norm, then Q, K, V for all positions, and bias, and gate 2. Kernel that computes the attention and output For kernel 2, we can have it also do the final gate multiply and output projection, if we load the gate and do the output proj in shared memory. Actually, for maximum fusion: we could even do the output projection in the attention kernel. After computing attention output (B, N, N, D), we multiply by gate (B, N, N, D) elementwise, then apply out_proj (linear on last dim), then add to the original pair_repr? Wait no, the reference returns just `self.out_proj(out)` where out is already gated. Actually the reference returns `self.out_proj(out)` where `out = g * (attention_output)`. So: - attention_output: (B, N, N, D) - g: (B, N, N, D) - gated = g * attention_output - result = Linear(gated) So we can fuse the output projection into the attention kernel as well. Better yet, we can compute gated * W_out in a very efficient way if we load the value vectors appropriately. But maybe it's simpler to write 2-3 kernels and let the compiler optimize. Let me think about the exact computation flow and see if we can do it all in one kernel or a few. Kernel "preprocess": - Input: pair_repr (B, N, N, D) - For each (b, i, j): - Compute layer norm over D dimension - Apply linear projections to get Q, K, V contributions, gate, bias - But Q, K, V are not per position in the way you'd think. For x_flat = x.reshape(B*N, N, D), each of the B*N "rows" has its own Q, K, V computed from all N positions in that flattened row. - So for flattened row index r = b*N + i, the Q for that row is W_q @ x[b, i, :, :].T? No. Wait, let me be more careful. x_flat is (B*N, N, D). For flattened row r = b*N + i, x_flat[r, :, :] = x[b, i, :, :]. Then: - q = self.query(x_flat) -- linear applied to each position independently - q[r, n, :] = W_q @ x_flat[r, n, :] = W_q @ x[b, i, n, :] So Q, K, V ARE per-position! The linear layers are applied independently to each token position. After the linear, we reshape into heads. So: for each (b, i, j) position in the original tensor, we compute: - q_raw[b, i, j, :] = W_q @ norm_x[b, i, j, :] - k_raw[b, i, j, :] = W_k @ norm_x[b, i, j, :] - v_raw[b, i, j, :] = W_v @ norm_x[b, i, j, :] - g[b, i, j, :] = sigmoid(W_g @ norm_x[b, i, j, :]) - bias_raw[b, i, j, :] = W_b @ norm_x[b, i, j, :] Then reshape q_raw, k_raw, v_raw into heads. This means the linear projections are indeed elementwise (applied per position) and we can compute them all in parallel! And bias[b, :, h, j, k]? Wait, bias is from bias_proj(x).permute(0, 3, 1, 2): bias[b, h, j, k] = bias_raw[b, j, k, h] (because permute(0, 3, 1, 2)) So bias is just the bias_proj output with permuted dimensions. OK so here's my kernel plan: **Kernel 1: Fused Preprocessing** - Each thread block handles a range of (b, i, j) positions - For each position: 1. Load pair_repr[b, i, j, :] (64 floats) 2. Compute mean and variance over D=64 3. Apply layer norm 4. Compute q, k, v, g, bias via matrix-vector multiplies with the weight matrices 5. Store results But computing 5 matrix-vector multiplies per position where each matrix is (out, in) with in=64 is a lot of computation. We can use registers efficiently. For D=64, we can have each thread process one position. A warp of 32 threads computing 32 positions in parallel. But the matrix-vector multiply with a 64x64 matrix needs cooperation to read the weight matrix efficiently. Actually, for a 64x64 matrix-vector multiply, the simplest approach is to have each thread compute the entire output vector for its position. The thread reads all 64 input elements, then for each output dimension, it reads the corresponding 64 weights and computes the dot product. This is 64*64 = 4096 multiplies per position, and we have 5 such matrices, so 20K multiplies per position. With 2048 positions, that's ~41M ops. Very manageable. The weights should be read from constant memory or cached L1. Since they're the same for all positions, they'll stay in cache. Wait, but for Q, K, V we compute 64-dimensional outputs, and for gate also 64, and for bias_proj we compute h=4. Total output per position: 64*4 + 4 = 260 floats. Let's think about the memory bandwidth: - Input: 2048 * 64 = 131K floats - Output Q, K, V: 2048 * 64 * 3 = 393K floats - Output gate: 2048 * 64 = 131K floats - Output bias: 2048 * 4 = 8K floats - Total output: ~532K floats This is small enough that compute might dominate. **Kernel 2: Fused Attention** Input: Q, K, V (each B*N, h, N, d), bias (B, h, N, N), pair_mask (B, N, N), gate (B, N, N, D) Output: out_proj result (B, N, N, D) Wait, we also need the output projection weights. For the attention, I need to compute for each (b, i, h, query_j): - scores[k] = sum_d(Q[b, i, h, query_j, d] * K[b, i, h, k, d]) / sqrt(d) + bias[b, h, query_j, k] Wait, let me check the bias indexing again. attn has shape (B, N, h, N, N) after reshape. The last two dims correspond to query position and key position? No... q is (B*N, h, N, d) where the N is the query positions (for that flattened row). k is (B*N, h, N, d) where N is the key positions. q @ k^T = (B*N, h, N, N) where dims are (queries, keys). Then view to (B, N, h, N, N). Here: - dim 0: batch - dim 1: row i (which flattened row) - dim 2: head - dim 3: query position j - dim 4: key position k And bias is (B, h, N, N), unsqueezed to (B, 1, h, N, N). So bias[b, h, j, k] is added to attn[b, i, h, j, k] for all i. And mask is (B, N, N), unsqueezed to (B, 1, 1, N, N). So mask[b, j, k] is applied to all heads and all query rows i. OK so to compute attention efficiently: For each flattened row r = b*N + i and head h: - For each query j in 0..N-1: - Compute scores[k] = dot(Q[r, h, j, :], K[r, h, k, :]) / sqrt(d) + bias[b, h, j, k] - Apply mask: if pair_mask[b, j, k] == 0, score = -inf - Softmax over k - Output[j, :] = sum_k softmax_score[k] * V[r, h, k, :] Then reshape output to (B, N, N, D), multiply by gate, apply output projection. Block configuration: - 32 threads (one per query) - Each thread loads its query vector Q[r, h, j, :] (16 floats) into registers - Collaborative loading of K[r, h, :, :] (32x16=512 floats) and V[r, h, :, :] (512 floats) into shared memory - Sync - Each thread computes its 32 attention scores by dotting its Q with each K in shared memory - Add bias and mask - Softmax over the 32 scores - Each thread computes its output vector by weighting the V vectors - Store output For bias and mask, we need bias[b, h, j, :] and pair_mask[b, j, :]. First, let me re-examine the problem to make sure I understand the shapes correctly: Input: pair_repr (B, N, N, D) Input: pair_mask (B, N, N) Forward: 1. x = layer_norm(pair_repr) # (B, N, N, D) 2. g = sigmoid(gate(x)) # (B, N, N, D) 3. bias = bias_proj(x).permute(0, 3, 1, 2) # (B, h, N, N) 4. x_flat = x.reshape(B*N, N, D) # (B*N, N, D) 5. q = query(x_flat).view(B*N, N, h, d).transpose(1, 2) # (B*N, h, N, d) 6. k = key(x_flat).view(B*N, N, h, d).transpose(1, 2) # (B*N, h, N, d) 7. v = value(x_flat).view(B*N, N, h, d).transpose(1, 2) # (B*N, h, N, d) 8. attn = (q @ k.transpose(-1, -2)) / sqrt(d) # (B*N, h, N, N) 9. attn = attn.view(B, N, h, N, N) 10. attn = attn + bias.unsqueeze(1) # bias_broadcast: (B, 1, h, N, N) 11. mask = pair_mask.unsqueeze(1).unsqueeze(2) # (B, 1, 1, N, N) 12. attn = attn.masked_fill(mask == 0, -inf) 13. attn = attn.view(B*N, h, N, N) 14. attn = softmax(attn, dim=-1) # over last dim (keys) 15. attn = dropout(attn) 16. out = (attn @ v).transpose(1, 2).reshape(B, N, N, D) # (B, N, N, D) 17. out = g * out 18. return out_proj(out) Wait, I need to be very careful about dropout. Now let me design the kernels concretely. **Kernel 1: preprocess** I'll create a kernel that takes pair_repr and computes: - q (B*N, h, N, d) - k (B*N, h, N, d) - v (B*N, h, N, d) - bias (B, h, N, N) - gate (B, N, N, D) For each (b, i, j) position: 1. How to organize finalize kernel: - Total output elements: B * N * N * D = 2 * 32 * 32 * 64 = 131072 - Block size: 256 threads - Number of blocks: ceil(131072 / 256) = 512 blocks - Each thread computes one output element: output[b, i, j, c] - Thread loads all 64 inputs for position (b, i, j) from scratch, multiplies by gate[b, i, j, :] (or scratch already has gate applied), and computes dot product with W_out[c, :] If scratch already has gate applied: each thread loads 64 floats, and W_out[c, :], computes 64 FMAs. Actually, the memory load of 64 floats per thread from scratch * 131K threads = 8.4M floats * 4 bytes = 33.6MB. But these are cached because consecutive threads read the same 64 floats (for the same position). With 256 threads in a block and each reading the same 64 floats, we read 64 floats from global memory once per block (they get cached in L1 and shared among threads). Wait, if 256 threads in a block all need the same 64 input floats, we should load them cooperatively to shared memory. Then each thread reads from shared memory to compute its output. This avoids redundant global memory loads and cache thrashing. So finalize kernel block: 1. Map block and thread to position and output channel 2. For the block's position(s): - Load scratch * gate (or just scratch if gate already applied) into shared memory - __syncthreads() - Each thread computes one output channel - Thread reads one row of W_out[c, :] (64 floats, not ideal for coalescing if c differs across threads) Hmm, reading W_out: if thread t computes output channel c = t, then thread 0 reads W_out[0, :], thread 1 reads W_out[1, :], etc. This is coalesced if W_out is row-major: consecutive threads read consecutive rows, but each row is 64 floats=256 bytes. So thread 0 reads bytes 0..255, thread 1 reads 256..511, etc. This is perfectly coalesced (consecutive threads read consecutive memory). So finalize kernel: - Block of 256 threads handles 1 position and computes all 256 of its 64 output channels? No, 64 channels. - If block has 256 threads and position has only 64 output channels, then 192 threads are idle for that position. - Better: block handles 4 positions, 64 threads per position. No, within the block. - Then each of the 64 threads computes one output channel: dot(W_out[c, :], input_shared[:]) Total FMAs per thread: 64. Very small, but 512 blocks gives decent parallelism. Actually, let me organize this differently. A block of 256 threads handles 4 positions. Each position needs 64 output channels. 4 positions * 64 channels = 256 outputs. Exactly 256 threads. Here's my concrete plan for the CUDA kernel file: ```python import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> // Kernel 1: preprocess - layer norm + QKV + gate + bias_proj // One thread per output element of some projection for some position // Actually, use one thread per position __global__ void preprocess_kernel( const float* pair_repr, const float* ln_weight, const float* ln_bias, const float* W_query, const float* W_key, const float* W_value, const float* W_gate, const float* b_gate, const float* W_bias, float* q, // (B*N, h, N, d) float* k, // (B*N, h, N, d) float* v, // (B*N, h, N, d) float* gate, // (B, N, N, D) float* bias, // (B, h, N, N) int B, int N, int D, int h, int d ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total_pos = B * N * N; if (idx >= total_pos) return; int b = idx / (N * N); int rem = idx % (N * N); int i = rem / N; int j = rem % N; // Load pair repr for this position float x[64]; // D=64 #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { x[c] = pair_repr[((b * N + i) * N + j) * D + c]; } // Layer norm float mean = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) mean += x[c]; mean /= D; float var = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { float diff = x[c] - mean; var += diff * diff; } var /= D; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { x[c] = (x[c] - mean) * inv_std * ln_weight[c] + ln_bias[c]; } // Projections // Q, K, V: no bias for (int head = 0; head < h; head++) { for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) { int out_c = head * d + dd; float qval = 0.0f; float kval = 0.0f; float vval = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { qval += W_query[out_c * D + c] * x[c]; kval += W_key[out_c * D + c] * x[c]; vval += W_value[out_c * D + c] * x[c]; } int r = b * N + i; q[((r * h + head) * N + j) * d + dd] = qval; k[((r * h + head) * N + j) * d + dd] = kval; v[((r * h + head) * N + j) * d + dd] = vval; } } // Gate projection: with bias #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { float gval = b_gate[c]; #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < D; ic++) { gval += W_gate[c * D + ic] * x[ic]; } gate[((b * N + i) * N + j) * D + c] = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gval)); } // Bias projection: no bias, then permute #pragma unroll for (int head = 0; head < h; head++) { float bval = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { bval += W_bias[head * D + c] * x[c]; } // bias[b, head, i, j] bias[((b * h + head) * N + i) * N + j] = bval; } } // Kernel 2: attention // One block per (b, i, h) // Block dims: (32, 8) or just 256 threads where each 8 threads handle 1 query __global__ void attention_kernel( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, const float* bias, const float* pair_mask, float* scratch, // (B, N, N, D) int B, int N, int D, int h, int d ) { int b = blockIdx.x / (N * h); int rem = blockIdx.x % (N * h); int i = rem / h; int head = rem % h; int r = b * N + i; // Each block handles one head // 256 threads: 32 queries * 8 threads per query int tid = threadIdx.x; int query = tid / 8; int t_in_q = tid % 8; // Each thread handles d/8 = 2 dimensions int d_start = t_in_q * (d / 8); int d_end = d_start + (d / 8); // Load Q for this query float q_vec[2]; // assuming d/8 = 2 for (int dd = 0; dd < d/8; dd++) { q_vec[dd] = q[((r * h + head) * N + query) * d + d_start + dd]; } // Collaborative load of K and V to shared memory // Each block loads K (N, d) and V (N, d) __shared__ float K_shared[32 * 16]; __shared__ float V_shared[32 * 16]; // 256 threads, 32*16*2 = 1024 floats to load // Each thread loads 4 floats for (int l = tid; l < N * d; l += blockDim.x) { K_shared[l] = k[((r * h + head) * N + 0) * d + l]; // wait, k is (B*N, h, N, d) } __syncthreads(); // need all K loaded before using for (int l = tid; l < N * d; l += blockDim.x) { V_shared[l] = v[((r * h + head) * N + 0) * d + l]; } __syncthreads(); // Compute attention scores for this query float scores[32]; for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int dd = 0; dd < d/8; dd++) { dot += q_vec[dd] * K_shared[key * d + d_start + dd]; } // Reduce within the 8 threads of the query // Use warp shuffle if they're in same warp, or shared memory // Since 8 threads per query and warp is 32 threads: // query 0 uses threads 0-7, which are in the same warp #pragma unroll for (int offset = 4; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { dot += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, offset); } // Only thread 0 in each query group has the full dot if (t_in_q == 0) { scores[key] = dot / sqrtf((float)d) + bias[((b * h + head) * N + query) * N + key]; // Apply mask if (pair_mask[(b * N + query) * N + key] == 0.0f) { scores[key] = -1e9f; // -inf approximation } } } // Softmax over scores // Only thread 0 does this float max_score = -1e9f; if (t_in_q == 0) { for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) { if (scores[key] > max_score) max_score = scores[key]; } float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) { scores[key] = expf(scores[key] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[key]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) { scores[key] *= inv_sum; } } __syncthreads(); // Make sure scores are ready // Broadcast scores to all threads in the query group // We can load from scores array in shared memory or have thread 0 broadcast float score_val[32]; if (t_in_q == 0) { for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) { // store to shared for broadcast } } } ``` Wait, this attention kernel is getting quite complex with the warp shuffles. Let me simplify it. Simplest approach: one thread per query, 32 threads per block. - Block handles one (b, i, h) head - 32 threads, each handles one query - 8 blocks per SM means 8 warps per SM. Not great but simpler to write. Let me write the simple version first. ```cuda __global__ void attention_kernel( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, const float* bias, const float* pair_mask, float* scratch, int B, int N, int D, int h, int d ) { int b = blockIdx.x / (N * h); int rem = blockIdx.x % (N * h); int i = rem / h; int head = rem % h; int r = b * N + i; int query = threadIdx.x; if (query >= N) return; __shared__ float K_shared[32 * 16]; __shared__ float V_shared[32 * 16]; // Load K and V for (int l = threadIdx.x; l < N * d; l += blockDim.x) { K_shared[l] = k[((r * h + head) * N) * d + l]; V_shared[l] = v[((r * h + head) * N) * d + l]; } __syncthreads(); // Load Q for this query float q_vec[16]; #pragma unroll for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) { q_vec[dd] = q[((r * h + head) * N + query) * d + dd]; } // Compute scores float scores[32]; float max_score = -1e9f; #pragma unroll for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) { float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) { dot += q_vec[dd] * K_shared[key * d + dd]; } float score = dot / sqrtf((float)d); score += bias[((b * h + head) * N + query) * N + key]; if (pair_mask[(b * N + query) * N + key] == 0.0f) { score = -1e9f; } scores[key] = score; if (score > max_score) max_score = score; } // Softmax float sum_exp = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) { scores[key] = expf(scores[key] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[key]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; #pragma unroll for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) { scores[key] *= inv_sum; } // Compute output float out_vec[16]; #pragma unroll for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) out_vec[dd] = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) { float s = scores[key]; #pragma unroll for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) { out_vec[dd] += s * V_shared[key * d + dd]; } } // Write to scratch #pragma unroll for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) { int out_idx = ((b * N + i) * N + query) * D + head * d + dd; scratch[out_idx] = out_vec[dd]; } } ``` This is clean. Each block is 32 threads, one per query. 256 blocks total. For N=32 and d=16, each thread loads: - Q: 16 floats from global - K/V: loaded cooperatively to shared memory - Bias: 32 floats from global (one per key) - Mask: 32 floats from global... wait, mask is pair_mask[(b * N + query) * N + key]. But pair_mask is (B, N, N). pair_mask[b*N + query] would be if it were flattened as (B*N, N). But pair_mask is (B, N, N). So the correct indexing is pair_mask[b * N * N + query * N + key]. Wait, let me check: pair_mask has shape (B, N, N). The mask in the reference is `pair_mask.unsqueeze(1).unsqueeze(2)` = (B, 1, 1, N, N). This means mask depends on (b, query_pos, key_pos). In my kernel, for batch b, query=query_pos, key=key_pos. So the mask value is pair_mask[b, query, key]. So: `pair_mask[b * N * N + query * N + key]` Actually, is the mask indexed as (B, query, key) or (B, row, key)? Let's re-check the reference: `mask = pair_mask.unsqueeze(1).unsqueeze(2) # (B, 1, 1, N, N)` In the 5D attention tensor (B, N, h, N, N), the last two dims correspond to... well, in the attention (before view), q @ k^T gives (B*N, h, N, N) where the last two are (query_pos_in_row, key_pos). Wait, `unsqueeze(1).unsqueeze(2)` on (B, N, N): - (B, N, N) -> unsqueeze(1) -> (B, 1, N, N) - -> unsqueeze(2) -> (B, 1, 1, N, N) So the last two N dims of mask correspond to the last two N dims of attn (query_pos and key_pos). OK, so my mask indexing is: pair_mask[b * N * N + query * N + key] which assumes pair_mask[b, query, key]. This is correct for the 5D tensor interpretation. And it's applied after the attention is reshaped to (B, N, h, N, N) where the last two dims are "query position" and "key position". So indeed, pair_mask[b, j, k] corresponds to query j and key k. Now for the finalize kernel: ```cuda __global__ void finalize_kernel( const float* scratch, const float* gate, const float* W_out, const float* b_out, float* output, int B, int N, int D ) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * N * N * D; if (idx >= total) return; int b = idx / (N * N * D); int rem = idx % (N * N * D); int i = rem / (N * D); rem = rem % (N * D); int j = rem / D; int c = rem % D; // Load gated input for this position float gated[64]; // D for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { gated[d] = scratch[((b * N + i) * N + j) * D + d] * gate[((b * N + i) * N + j) * D + d]; } // Output projection float out_val = b_out[c]; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { out_val += W_out[c * D + d] * gated[d]; } output[((b * N + i) * N + j) * D + c] = out_val; } ``` Wait, this loads all D inputs for each thread, which is very inefficient. Better to organize by position. Let me rewrite finalize so each block handles one position and all threads cooperate: ```cuda __global__ void finalize_kernel( const float* scratch, const float* gate, const float* W_out, const float* b_out, float* output, int B, int N, int D ) { int pos = blockIdx.x; int total_pos = B * N * N; if (pos >= total_pos) return; int b = pos / (N * N); int rem = pos % (N * N); int i = rem / N; int j = rem % N; __shared__ float gated_shared[64]; // Load scratch * gate into shared memory for (int l = threadIdx.x; l < D; l += blockDim.x) { gated_shared[l] = scratch[pos * D + l] * gate[pos * D + l]; } __syncthreads(); // Each thread computes one output channel for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < D; c += blockDim.x) { float out_val = b_out[c]; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { out_val += W_out[c * D + d] * gated_shared[d]; } output[pos * D + c] = out_val; } } ``` Block size: 64 threads. Total blocks: 2048 positions. 2048 blocks of 64 threads = 131K threads. Hmm, 2048 blocks is good! Each block handles one position. 64 threads compute the 64x64 matvec. Each block loads 64 floats (gated_shared), each thread loads 1 float. Then each of the 64 threads computes 1 output channel, needing to read 64 weight floats and 64 shared floats. The weight reads are coalesced across threads (each thread reads a different row of W_out). The shared reads are the same for all threads, so served from shared memory directly. 2048 blocks of 64 threads. Total threads: 131K. Total warps: 4096. 4096 / 132 = 31 warps per SM. This is excellent occupancy! Wait, actually each block only has 64 threads = 2 warps. 2048 blocks * 2 warps = 4096 warps. Each SM can hold ~16 warps (or more). So 4096 / 132 = 31 warps per SM, which is good but well within the capacity. Now, let me put this all together. Actually, one more thought: the reference does `x.reshape(B * N, N, D)` then linear layers. PyTorch linear on a 2D tensor is essentially a matrix multiply. For (B*N, N, D) @ (D, D), this is a batched GEMM where each "batch" is a row of the first N dimension. But x_flat is (64, 32, 64), and linear applies to the last dim, giving (64, 32, 64). In PyTorch, this is very efficient - it calls cuBLAS GEMM with M=64*32=2048, K=64, N=64. A single GEMM of size (2048, 64, 64). This is super fast in cuBLAS. For Q @ K^T, the reference does q (64, 4, 32, 16) @ k (64, 4, 32, 16).transpose(-1, -2) = (64, 4, 32, 32). In PyTorch, this is a batched matmul: batch=64*4=256, M=32, K=16, N=32. Also efficient. Then attn @ v: (64, 4, 32, 32) @ v (64, 4, 32, 16) = (64, 4, 32, 16). Batch=256, M=32, K=32, N=16. These are all efficiently handled by cuBLAS. The main overhead is: 1. The reshape, view, transpose, unsqueeze operations (memory copies or view ops) 2. The multiple kernel launches 3. Writing intermediate results to global memory A fused kernel that avoids the intermediate writes should beat the reference even if the compute isn't perfectly optimal. Now let me think about whether my custom attention kernel (32 threads per block, 256 blocks) with loops over 32 scores and 32 keys will actually be faster than cuBLAS batched matmul + softmax kernel + elementwise ops + another matmul. For N=32, d=16, a custom kernel that keeps everything in registers/shared memory might win because: 1. cuBLAS might not be perfectly optimized for 32x32 matrices 2. The custom kernel fuses QK^T + bias + mask + softmax + @V into a single kernel 3. No intermediate global memory writes for the attention scores For the batched matmuls: - QK^T: 256 batches of (32, 16) @ (16, 32). M=32, K=16, N=32. - attn @ V: 256 batches of (32, 32) @ (32, 16). M=32, K=32, N=16. These are tiny for cuBLAS. The standard cuBLAS might not even use Tensor Cores for these sizes, and the kernel launch overhead for each batch can be significant. So a single custom kernel for attention is likely to win. Similarly, for the linear projections in preprocess: the reference uses a single matmul of size (2048, 64) @ (64, 64) for each linear layer. That's actually quite efficient. My custom kernel computing these projections position-by-position might not beat cuBLAS. To beat the reference, the fused approach just needs to be faster than the reference's sequence of: 1. layer_norm (kernel) 2. 5x linear (5 kernels/GEMMs) 3. QK^T (1 kernel/GEMM) 4. reshape, add, mask (2-3 kernels) 5. softmax (1 kernel) 6. @V (1 kernel/GEMM) 7. reshape, multiply, linear (3 kernels) By fusing into 2-3 kernels, we eliminate most kernel launch overhead and intermediate memory writes. Even if each custom kernel is slightly less efficient than cuBLAS for the GEMM part, the fusion wins. Very fast for one thread. - Then softmax - Then compute output: 32 keys * 16 dims = 512 FMAs - Warp j writes output for query j - 4 heads * 32 warps = 128 "query-head" computations, but we only have 32 warps. - We need to do 4 heads sequentially, or parallelize differently. With 32 warps handling 32 queries, we can process one head at a time. All 32 warps handle the 32 queries for head 0, then head 1, etc. 4 iterations. In each iteration: - Load K and V for the head into shared memory (or they might already be there from projection phase) - Each warp computes attention for its query - Write output After all 4 heads, we have the full attention output in shared memory (32 * 64 = 2KB). 6. Output phase: - Apply gate: elementwise multiply - Apply output projection: (32, 64) @ (64, 64) - This is 32 positions * 64 outputs = 2048 outputs - 32 warps * 32 threads = 1024 threads - Each thread computes 2 outputs: each is a 64-dim dot product - Total: 2048 * 64 = 131K FMAs - Write to global output This single kernel does EVERYTHING. 64 blocks of 1024 threads = 65,536 threads = 2048 warps. 2048 / 132 = 15.5 warps per SM. Good occupancy. Shared memory usage: - x: 32 * 64 = 2KB - Q/K/V for one head: 32 * 16 * 3 = 1.5KB (can reuse) - Attention output (all heads): 32 * 64 = 2KB - Gate: 32 * 64 = 2KB - Temp for softmax scores: 32 floats per warp? Or shared across block: 32 queries * 32 keys = 1KB - We don't need all 32*32 scores in shared memory simultaneously if each warp computes its own query's scores in registers. Total shared memory: ~8KB. Very manageable. The main concern is whether I can actually write this kernel correctly and efficiently. It's quite complex. And with 64 blocks, each with 1024 threads doing a LOT of work, there might be register pressure. Actually, writing this as a single kernel is very challenging and error-prone for a first attempt. Let me write the simpler 3-kernel version first. --- Actually, I've spent a lot of time analyzing. Let me write the code now. I'll go with: 1. `preprocess_kernel`: 2048 blocks of 64 threads. Each block handles one position, threads cooperate on output projection. Wait, I just realized: for attention kernel with 32 threads, N=32, each thread handles one query. For each thread: - It needs to read Q_j (16 floats), K_all (512 floats... from shared), V_all (512 floats from shared), bias_row (32 floats), mask_row (32 floats). - But K_all and V_all are read to shared memory collaboratively. If 32 threads each load 16 K floats and 16 V floats, that's 512 + 512 = 1024 floats. Each thread loads 32 floats. No. Let me just have each thread load one key's worth of K and V: - Thread t loads K[t, :] (16 floats) and V[t, :] (16 floats) for t=0..31. - This is perfectly coalesced if the inner dim (d=16) is contiguous. - Total per thread: 32 floats. Total per block: 1024 floats. Then in shared memory: - K_shared[t, :] = K[t, :] for t=0..31 - V_shared[t, :] = V[t, :] for t=0..31 Great! Then each thread computes scores[j] for its query j by reading all K_shared[k, :] for k=0..31. Each thread accesses all 32 keys, reading 16 floats per key = 512 floats from shared memory. No bank conflicts if K_shared[k, d] is stored with d as the inner dimension and consecutive d values in consecutive banks. Actually, if all 32 threads read K_shared[k, :] for the SAME k simultaneously, that's a broadcast (no conflict). If they read different k, it's strided access which might have bank conflicts depending on the stride. In our case, when computing scores, thread j computes dot(Q_j, K_k) for all k sequentially. So at step k, all 32 threads read K_shared[k, :] for the SAME k. This is a broadcast from shared memory - no bank conflicts! Perfect. Then for the output computation, thread j computes sum_k score[k] * V[k, :] for its query j. It accesses V[k, :] sequentially for k=0..31. Again, at each step k, all threads read the SAME V[k, :]. Perfect broadcast! This is an ideal access pattern for shared memory. And for Q_j and the final output, each thread reads/writes its own data with no conflicts. So the attention kernel with 32 threads per block is actually very well-suited for this problem! Let me write it. Now, writing the code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = """ #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __global__ void preprocess_kernel( const float* __restrict__ pair_repr, const float* __restrict__ ln_weight, const float* __restrict__ ln_bias, const float* __restrict__ W_query, const float* __restrict__ W_key, const float* __restrict__ W_value, const float* __restrict__ W_gate, const float* __restrict__ b_gate, const float* __restrict__ W_bias, float* __restrict__ q, float* __restrict__ k, float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ gate, float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int N, int D, int h, int d ) { int pos = blockIdx.x; int total_pos = B * N * N; if (pos >= total_pos) return; int b = pos / (N * N); int rem = pos % (N * N); int i = rem / N; int j = rem % N; // Load input float x[64]; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { x[c] = pair_repr[pos * D + c]; } // Layer norm float mean = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) mean += x[c]; mean /= D; float var = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { float diff = x[c] - mean; var += diff * diff; } var /= D; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { x[c] = (x[c] - mean) * inv_std * ln_weight[c] + ln_bias[c]; } // Query, Key, Value projections (no bias) int r = b * N + i; for (int head = 0; head < h; head++) { for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) { int out_c = head * d + dd; float qval = 0.0f, kval = 0.0f, vval = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { float xc = x[c]; qval += W_query[out_c * D + c] * xc; kval += W_key[out_c * D + c] * xc; vval += W_value[out_c * D + c] * xc; } int idx = ((r * h + head) * N + j) * d + dd; q[idx] = qval; k[idx] = kval; v[idx] = vval; } } // Gate projection (with bias) #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { float gval = b_gate[c]; #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < D; ic++) { gval += W_gate[c * D + ic] * x[ic]; } gate[pos * D + c] = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gval)); } // Bias projection (no bias), then permute #pragma unroll for (int head = 0; head < h; head++) { float bval = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { bval += W_bias[head * D + c] * x[c]; } bias[((b * h + head) * N + i) * N + j] = bval; } } __global__ void attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ pair_mask, float* __restrict__ scratch, int B, int N, int D, int h, int d ) { int b = blockIdx.x / (N * h); int rem = blockIdx.x % (N * h); int i = rem / h; int head = rem % h; int r = b * N + i; int query = threadIdx.x; if (query >= N) return; __shared__ float K_shared[32 * 16]; __shared__ float V_shared[32 * 16]; // Collaborative load of K and V // Thread t loads K[t, :] and V[t, :] for (int l = query; l < N * d; l += blockDim.x) { K_shared[l] = k[((r * h + head) * N) * d + l]; V_shared[l] = v[((r * h + head) * N) * d + l]; } __syncthreads(); // Load Q for this query float q_vec[16]; #pragma unroll for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) { q_vec[dd] = q[((r * h + head) * N + query) * d + dd]; } // Compute attention scores float scores[32]; float max_score = -1e9f; #pragma unroll for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) { float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) { dot += q_vec[dd] * K_shared[key * d + dd]; } float score = dot / sqrtf((float)d); score += bias[((b * h + head) * N + query) * N + key]; // Apply mask if (pair_mask[(b * N + query) * N + key] == 0.0f) { score = -1e9f; } scores[key] = score; if (score > max_score) max_score = score; } // Softmax float sum_exp = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) { scores[key] = expf(scores[key] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[key]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; #pragma unroll for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) { scores[key] *= inv_sum; } // Compute attention output float out_vec[16]; #pragma unroll for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) out_vec[dd] = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) { float s = scores[key]; #pragma unroll for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) { out_vec[dd] += s * V_shared[key * d + dd]; } } // Write to scratch #pragma unroll for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) { int out_idx = ((b * N + i) * N + query) * D + head * d + dd; scratch[out_idx] = out_vec[dd]; } } __global__ void finalize_kernel( const float* __restrict__ scratch, const float* __restrict__ gate, const float* __restrict__ W_out, const float* __restrict__ b_out, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int N, int D ) { int pos = blockIdx.x; int total_pos = B * N * N; if (pos >= total_pos) return; __shared__ float gated_shared[64]; // Load scratch * gate into shared memory for (int l = threadIdx.x; l < D; l += blockDim.x) { gated_shared[l] = scratch[pos * D + l] * gate[pos * D + l]; } __syncthreads(); // Each thread computes some output channels for (int c = threadIdx.x; c < D; c += blockDim.x) { "attention_kernel", "finalize_kernel"] class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, pair_dim, num_heads, dropout=0.0): super().__init__() self.num_heads = num_heads self.head_dim = pair_dim // pair_dim, bias=False) self.gate = nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim) self.bias_proj = nn.Linear(pair_dim, num_heads, bias=False) self.out_proj = nn.Linear(pair_dim, pair_dim) self.dropout = nn.Dropout(dropout) # Compile CUDA extension self.cuda_module = load_inline( name="evoformer_attention", cpp_sources="", # no cpp needed cuda_sources=CUDA_SOURCE, functions=functions, extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3", "--use_fast_math"], verbose=False, ) def forward(self, pair_repr, pair_mask): B, N, _, D = pair_repr.shape h = self.num_heads d = self.head_dim device = pair_repr.device # Allocate outputs q = torch.empty(B * N, h, N, d, device=device, dtype=torch.float32) k = torch.empty(B * N, h, N, d, device=device, dtype=torch.float32) v = torch.empty(B * N, h, N, d, device=device, dtype=torch.float32) gate = torch.empty(B, N, N, D, device=device, dtype=torch.float32) bias = torch.empty(B, h, N, N, device=device, dtype=torch.float32) scratch = torch.empty(B, N, N, D, device=device, dtype=torch.float32) output = torch.empty(B, N, N, D, device=device, dtype=torch.float32) # Launch preprocess kernel self.cuda_module.preprocess_kernel( pair_repr, self.layer_norm.weight, self.layer_norm.bias, self.query.weight, self.key.weight, self.value.weight, self.gate.weight, self.gate.bias, self.bias_proj.weight, q, k, v, gate, bias, B, N, D, h, d ) # For attention: we have 32 queries and 4 heads = 128 attention computations. Each computation needs QK^T, softmax, and @V. Queries 8-15 in warp 1. Queries 16-23 in warp 2. Queries 24-31 in warp 3. For query j, the 4 threads are contiguous within a warp. Great! So we can use warp shuffle for reduction within the 4 threads? Well, `__shfl_down_sync` operates on a warp. If we want to reduce only within threads j*4 to j*4+3, we can use `__shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, 1)` but that gets values from all threads in the warp, not just the 4 for our query. To reduce properly, we might need to mask: ```cpp int lane = threadIdx.x % 32; int query_in_warp = lane / 4; int tid_in_query = lane % 4; float val = ...; #pragma unroll for (int offset = 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } ``` But this reduces across ALL 32 threads in the warp, not just the 4 for our query. Threads from other queries would contribute wrong values. To fix this, we can either: 1. Use __shfl_down_sync with a proper mask for each query group, or 2. Use shared memory for reduction. For shared memory reduction: - Each thread writes its partial dot to shared_mem[threadIdx.x] - __syncthreads() - For each query, one thread (e.g., the first of the 4) reads the 4 values and sums them. This is simple and doesn't depend on warp layout. Shared memory for reduction: 512 floats. Very small. Let me write the full Python module: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __global__ void preprocess_kernel( const float* __restrict__ pair_repr, const float* __restrict__ ln_weight, const float* __restrict__ ln_bias, const float* __restrict__ W_query, const float* __restrict__ W_key, const float* __restrict__ W_value, const float* __restrict__ W_gate, const float* __restrict__ b_gate, const float* __restrict__ W_bias, float* __restrict__ q, float* __restrict__ k, float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ gate, float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int N, int D, int h, int d ) { int pos = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; c < D; c++) mean += x[c]; mean /= D; float var = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { float diff = x[c] - mean; var += diff * diff; } var /= D; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { x[c] = (x[c] - mean) * inv_std * ln_weight[c] + ln_bias[c]; } // Extract indices int b = pos / (N * N); int rem = pos % (N * N); int i = rem / N; int j = rem % N; int r = b * N + i; // Query, Key, Value (no bias) for (int head = 0; head < h; head++) { for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) { int out_c = head * d + dd; float qval = 0.0f, kval = 0.0f, vval = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { float xc = x[c]; qval += W_query[out_c * D + c] * xc; kval += W_key[out_c * D + c] * xc; vval += W_value[out_c * D + c] * xc; } int idx = ((r * h + head) * N + j) * d + dd; q[idx] = qval; k[idx] = kval; v[idx] = vval; } } // Gate (with bias) #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { float gval = b_gate[c]; #pragma unroll for (int ic = 0; ic < D; ic++) { gval += W_gate[c * D + ic] * x[ic]; } gate[pos * D + c] = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-gval)); } // Bias proj (no bias), permute to (B, h, N, N) #pragma unroll for (int head = 0; head < h; head++) { float bval = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { bval += W_bias[head * D + c] * x[c]; } bias[((b * h + head) * N + i) * N + j] = bval; } } __global__ void attention_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ pair_mask, float* __restrict__ scratch, int B, int N, int D, int h, int d ) { int b = blockIdx.x / (N * h); int rem = blockIdx.x % (N * h); int i = rem / h; int head = rem % h; int r = b * N + i; int query = threadIdx.x; if (query >= N) return; extern __shared__ float shared_mem[]; float* K_shared = shared_mem; float* V_shared = shared_mem + N * d; // Each thread loads one key's K and V for (int l = query; l < N * d; l += blockDim.x) { K_shared[l] = k[((r * h + head) * N) * d + l]; V_shared[l] = v[((r * h + head) * N) * d + l]; int threads = 64; int blocks = (total_pos + threads - 1) / threads; preprocess_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( pair_repr.data_ptr<float>(), ln_weight.data_ptr<float>(), ln_bias.data_ptr<float>(), W_query.data_ptr<float>(), W_key.data_ptr<float>(), W_value.data_ptr<float>(), W_gate.data_ptr<float>(), b_gate.data_ptr<float>(), W_bias.data_ptr<float>(), q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), gate.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), B, N, D, h, d ); } void launch_finalize( torch::Tensor scratch, torch::Tensor gate, torch::Tensor W_out, torch::Tensor b_out, torch::Tensor output, int B, int N, int D ) { int blocks = B * N * N; int threads = 64; finalize_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( scratch.data_ptr<float>(), gate.data_ptr<float>(), W_out.data_ptr<float>(), b_out.data_ptr<float>(), output.data_ptr<float>(), B, N, D ); Let me also consider: what if I skip writing the custom preprocess and just use PyTorch's operations for layer norm and the linear layers, but fuse the attention and output projection? ```python def forward(self, pair_repr, pair_mask): B, N, _, D = pair_repr.shape h = self.num_heads d = self.head_dim x = self.layer_norm(pair_repr) g = torch.sigmoid(self.gate(x)) bias = self.bias_proj(x).permute(0, 3, 1, 2) x_flat = x.reshape(B * N, N, D) q = self.query(x_flat).view(B * N, N, h, d).transpose(1, 2) k = self.key(x_flat).view(B * N, N, h, d).transpose(1, 2) v = self.value(x_flat).view(B * N, N, h, d).transpose(1, 2) # Custom attention kernel scratch = torch.empty(B, N, N, D, device=pair_repr.device) self.cuda_module.launch_attention(q, k, v, bias, pair_mask, scratch, B, N, D, h, d) # Custom finalize kernel output = torch.empty(B, N, N, D, device=pair_repr.device) self.cuda_module.launch_finalize(scratch, g, self.out_proj.weight, self.out_proj.bias, output, B, N, D) return output ``` This mixes PyTorch ops with custom kernels. Let me write the preprocess kernel with 64 threads per block, one block per position. Preprocess kernel C++: ```cpp __global__ void preprocess_kernel( const float* __restrict__ pair_repr, const float* __restrict__ ln_weight, const float* __restrict__ ln_bias, const float* __restrict__ W_query, const float* __restrict__ W_key, const float* __restrict__ W_value, const float* __restrict__ W_gate, const float* __restrict__ b_gate, const float* __restrict__ W_bias, float* __restrict__ q, float* __restrict__ k, float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ gate, float* __restrict__ bias, int B, int N, int D, int h, int d ) { int pos = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; if (tid >= D) return; // safety, though blockDim.x should be >= D __shared__ float x_shared[128]; __shared__ float reduce_buf[128]; // Load channel tid x_shared[tid] = pair_repr[pos * D + tid]; __syncthreads(); // Compute mean reduce_buf[tid] = x_shared[tid]; __syncthreads(); // Tree reduction for sum for (int stride = D / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { if (tid < stride) { reduce_buf[tid] += reduce_buf[tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float mean = reduce_buf[0] / D; // Compute variance reduce_buf[tid] = (x_shared[tid] - mean) * (x_shared[tid] - mean); __syncthreads(); for (int stride = D / 2; stride > 0; stride /= 2) { if (tid < stride) { reduce_buf[tid] += reduce_buf[tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float var = reduce_buf[0] / D; // Normalize float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f); x_shared[tid] = (x_shared[tid] - mean) * inv_std * ln_weight[tid] + ln_bias[tid]; __syncthreads(); // Now compute projections // Each thread handles some outputs int b = pos / (N * N); int rem = pos % (N * N); int i = rem / N; int j = rem % N; int r = b * N + i; int total_outputs = 4 * D + h; // Q + K + V + gate + bias for (int out_idx = tid; out_idx < total_outputs; out_idx += blockDim.x) { // Determine which projection and which output channel float val = 0.0f; int out_c; bool has_bias = false; float bias_val = 0.0f; bool is_sigmoid = false; if (out_idx < D) { // Q out_c = out_idx; // no bias } else if (out_idx < 2 * D) { // K out_c = out_idx - D; } else if (out_idx < 3 * D) { // V out_c = out_idx - 2 * D; } else if (out_idx < 4 * D) { // Gate out_c = out_idx - 3 * D; has_bias = true; bias_val = b_gate[out_c]; is_sigmoid = true; } else { // Bias proj out_c = out_idx - 4 * D; // head index } // Compute dot product with x_shared if (out_idx < 4 * D) { for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { if (out_idx < D) { val += W_query[out_c * D + c] * x_shared[c]; } else if (out_idx < 2 * D) { val += W_key[(out_idx - D) * D + c] * x_shared[c]; } else if (out_idx < 3 * D) { val += W_value[(out_idx - 2 * D) * D + c] * x_shared[c]; } else { val += W_gate[(out_idx - 3 * D) * D + c] * x_shared[c]; } } } else { for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) { val += W_bias[out_c * D + c] * x_shared[c]; } } if (has_bias) val += bias_val; if (is_sigmoid) val = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-val)); // Write to output if (out_idx < D) { int head = out_c / d; int dd = out_c % d; q[((r * h + head) * N + j) * d + dd] = val; } else if (out_idx < 2 * D) { int head = (out_idx - D) / d; int dd = (out_idx - D) % d; k[((r * h + head) * N + j) * d + dd] = val; } else if (out_idx < 3 * D) { int head = (out_idx - 2 * D) / d; int dd = (out_idx - 2 * D) % d; v[((r * h + head) * N + j) * d + dd] = val; } else if (out_idx < 4 * D) { gate[pos * D + (out_idx - 3 * D)] = val; } else { bias[((b * h + out_c) * N + i) * N + j] = val; } } } ``` This is actually quite clean! But the branch `if (out_idx < D) ... else if ...` inside the computation loop is ugly. Let me restructure it. Actually, there's a simpler way: since all projections use different weight matrices, and the output channels have different mappings, I can just have separate loops for each projection. But with 64 threads and 5 projections (260 outputs total), if each thread handles 4 outputs, the natural approach is: ```cpp // Q projection: D outputs (64) for (int out_c = tid; out_c < D; out_c += blockDim.x) { float val = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) val += W_query[out_c * D + c] * x_shared[c]; int head = out_c / d; int dd = out_c % d; q[((r * h + head) * N + j) * d + dd] = val; } // K projection: D outputs for (int out_c = tid; out_c < D; out_c += blockDim.x) { float val = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) val += W_key[out_c * D + c] * x_shared[c]; int head = out_c / d; int dd = out_c % d; k[((r * h + head) * N + j) * d + dd] = val; } // Same for V, gate, bias ``` This is much cleaner! And since D=64 and blockDim.x=64, each loop executes exactly once per thread (no stepping needed). For gate: ```cpp for (int out_c = tid; out_c < D; out_c += blockDim.x) { float val = b_gate[out_c]; for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) val += W_gate[out_c * D + c] * x_shared[c]; gate[pos * D + out_c] = 1.0f / (1.0f + expf(-val)); } ``` For bias: ```cpp for (int out_c = tid; out_c < h; out_c += blockDim.x) { float val = 0.0f; for (int c = 0; c < D; c++) val += W_bias[out_c * D + c] * x_shared[c]; bias[((b * h + out_c) * N + i) * N + j] = val; } ``` This is clean and branch-free! Each thread executes exactly one iteration of each loop. Total FMAs per thread: - Q: 64 - K: 64 - V: 64 - Gate: 64 - Bias: 64 (but only first 4 threads do this, others are idle) - Total active FMAs per thread: ~256 With 64 threads * 256 FMAs = 16K FMAs per block. 2048 blocks = 33M FMAs. h;\n int head = rem % h;\n int r = b * N + i;\n int query = threadIdx.x;\n \n if (query >= N) return;\n \n extern __shared__ float shared_mem2[];\n float* K_shared = shared_mem2;\n float* V_shared = shared_mem2 + N * d;\n \n // Load K and V of shape (N, d)\n for (int l = query; l < N * d; l += blockDim.x) {\n K_shared[l] = k[((r * h + head) * N) * d + l];\n V_shared[l] = v[((r * h + head) * N) * d + l];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n // Load Q\n float q_local[64];\n for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) {\n q_local[dd] = q[((r * h + head) * N + query) * d + dd];\n }\n \n float scores[64];\n float max_score = -1e9f;\n \n for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) {\n float dot = 0.0f;\n for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) {\n dot += q_local[dd] * K_shared[key * d + dd];\n }\n float score = dot / sqrtf((float)d);\n score += bias[((b * h + head) * N + query) * N + key];\n \n if (pair_mask[(b * N + query) * N + key] == 0.0f) {\n score = -1e9f;\n }\n \n scores[key] = score;\n if (score > max_score) max_score = score;\n }\n \n float sum_exp = 0.0f;\n for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) {\n scores[key] = expf(scores[key] - max_score);\n sum_exp += scores[key];\n }\n float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp;\n for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) {\n scores[key] *= inv_sum;\n }\n \n float out_local[64];\n for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) out_local[dd] = 0.0f;\n \n for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) {\n float s = scores[key];\n for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) {\n out_local[dd] += s * V_shared[key * d + dd];\n }\n }\n \n for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) {\n int out_idx = ((b * N + i) * N + query) * D + head * d + dd;\n scratch[out_idx] = out_local[dd];\n }\n}\n\n__global__ void finalize_kernel(\n const float* __restrict__ scratch,\n const float* __restrict__ gate,\n const float* __restrict__ W_out,\n const float* __restrict__ b_out,\n float* __restrict__ output,\n int B, int N, int D\n) {\n int pos = blockIdx.x;\n int tid = threadIdx.x;\n \n extern __shared__ float s3[];\n float* gated_shared = s3;\n \n if (tid < D) {\n gated_shared[tid] = scratch[pos * D + tid] * gate[pos * D + tid];\n }\n __syncthreads();\n \n for (int c = tid; c < D; c += blockDim.x) {\n float out_val = b_out[c];\n for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) {\n out_val += W_out[c * D + d] * gated_shared[d];\n }\n output[pos * D + c] = out_val;\n }\n}\n\nvoid launch_preprocess(\n torch::Tensor pair_repr, torch::Tensor ln_weight, torch::Tensor ln_bias,\n torch::Tensor W_query, torch::Tensor W_key, torch::Tensor W_value,\n torch::Tensor W_gate, torch::Tensor b_gate, torch::Tensor W_bias,\n torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v,\n torch::Tensor gate, torch::Tensor bias,\n int B, int N, int D, int h, int d\n) {\n int total_pos = B * N * N;\n int smem = 256 * sizeof(float);\n preprocess_kernel<<<total_pos, 64, smem>>>(\n pair_repr.data_ptr<float>(), ln_weight.data_ptr<float>(), ln_bias.data_ptr<float>(),\n W_query.data_ptr<float>(), W_key.data_ptr<float>(), W_value.data_ptr<float>(),\n W_gate.data_ptr<float>(), b_gate.data_ptr<float>(), W_bias.data_ptr<float>(),\n q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(),\n gate.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(),\n B, N, D, h, d\n );\n}\n\nvoid launch_attention(\n torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v,\n torch::Tensor bias, torch::Tensor pair_mask,\n torch::Tensor scratch,\n int B, int N, int D, int h, int d\n) {\n int blocks = B * N * h;\n int threads = N;\n int smem = 2 * N * d * sizeof(float);\n attention_kernel<<<blocks, threads, smem>>>(\n q.data_ptr<float>(), ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, pair_dim, num_heads, dropout=0.0):\n super().__init__()\n self.num_heads = num_heads\n self.head_dim = pair_dim // num_heads\n \n self.layer_norm = Each thread handles one tile? No, 256 threads for 1024 elements. - Divide into tiles of 16×16 = 256 elements. For a 16×16 tile, 64 threads: - Row r = threadIdx.x / 16 (0..3 within tile) - Col c = threadIdx.x % 16 - Actually, 64 threads for 16×16 = 256 elements. Each thread handles 4 elements. - Element assignment: (r0, c0), (r0+4, c0), (r0+8, c0), (r0+12, c0) where r0 = (tid % 64) / 16, c0 = tid % 16... Hmm, this gets complicated. I think the cleanest is: - 256 threads, each handles (query, key) pairs spaced by 256. - Thread tid handles pairs: tid, tid+256, tid+512, tid+768. - For each pair (q, k): score = dot(Q[q], K[k]) + bias[b, head, i, k] - Q[q] loaded into registers (temporarily, per pair). To load Q efficiently: each of the 32 queries' Q vectors is needed by 32 threads (one per key). If I load Q into shared memory, all 32 threads can read it. Q shared memory: 32 * 16 = 512 floats = 2KB. Total shared memory: K(2KB) + V(2KB) + Q(2KB) + scores(4KB) = 10KB. Kernel steps: 1. Load K, V into shared memory 2. Load Q into shared memory (all 32 queries × 16 channels) 3. All threads compute their assigned scores: - For pair (q, k): read Q[q] and K[k] from shared memory, compute dot product - Each thread computes 4 scores 4. Write scores to global/shared memory 5. Do softmax (complex with this thread layout) 6. Compute output Actually, step 5 (softmax) is hard if scores are distributed. If I put scores in shared memory, I can do softmax there. But with 4KB for scores, I need all 1024 scores in shared memory. After computing scores, each thread writes to `scores[q * N + k]`. Then __syncthreads(). Now all scores are in shared memory. Then for softmax, threads 0..31 (one per query) can do the max/sum normalizations. Each thread loops over 32 keys. Then for output, threads compute out[q][c] using scores and V. Thread assignment for output: 256 threads, 512 outputs (32 queries × 16 channels). Each thread computes 2 outputs. ```c int out_base = tid * 2; int q0 = out_base / 16; int c0 = out_base % 16; int q1 = q0 + 1; // or handle differently ``` Actually, 256 threads for 512 outputs = 2 each. Each thread computes: - output[q0][c0] and output[q1][c1] Where q0 = (tid % 128) / 16 = 0..7, c0 = tid % 16 = 0..15 Then q1 = q0 + 8, c1 = c0. So thread tid handles (query, channel) pairs: - (q0, c0) and (q0+8, c0) where q0 = (tid % 128) / 16, c0 = tid % 16. Each thread computes 2 outputs: ```c float val0 = 0, val1 = 0; for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) { float s0 = scores[q0 * N + key]; float s1 = scores[(q0 + 8) * N + key]; float v_k = V_s[key * 16 + c0]; val0 += s0 * v_k; val1 += s1 * v_k; } ``` Then each thread writes 2 values to scratch. This tile-based attention with 256 threads per block seems very promising! Let me write it out carefully. But the complexity is high and there might be bugs. Let me try something simpler first. Simpler approach: in the current attention kernel, just change from 32 threads to 64 threads per block, with each block still handling 1 head-row but using multiple threads per query. With 64 threads and 32 queries, each query gets 2 threads. The 2 threads split the d=16 dot product: - Thread 0: channels 0..7 - Thread 1: channels 8..15 After computing partials, reduce via warp shuffle or shared memory. For 64 threads: - 2 threads per query - 32 queries per head-row - 64 threads per block - Blocks = B*N*h = 256 - Total warps = 256 * 2 = 512 - Warps per SM = 512/132 ≈ 3.9 Better! And not too complex. Let me implement this: ```c __global__ void attention_kernel_64( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, const float* bias, const float* pair_mask, float* scratch, int B, int N, int D, int h, int d ) { int b = blockIdx.x / (N * h); int rem = blockIdx.x % (N * h); int i = rem / h; int head = rem % h; int r = b * N + i; int tid = threadIdx.x; int query = tid / 2; // 0..31 int t = tid % 2; // 0..1 (which half of channels) int d_half = d / 2; // 8 extern __shared__ float shared[]; float* K_s = shared; float* V_s = shared + N * d; // Load K and V for (int l = tid; l < N * d; l += blockDim.x) { K_s[l] = k[((r * h + head) * N) * d + l]; V_s[l] = v[((r * h + head) * N) * d + l]; } __syncthreads(); // Load Q for this query (all 16 channels) float q_reg[16]; for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) { q_reg[dd] = q[((r * h + head) * N + query) * d + dd]; } // Compute scores for this query float scores[32]; float max_score = -1e9f; for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) { float dot = 0.0f; for (int dd = t * d_half; dd < (t + 1) * d_half; dd++) { dot += q_reg[dd] * K_s[key * d + dd]; } // Reduce with the other thread for same query dot += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, 1); float score = dot / sqrtf((float)d); score += bias[((b * h + head) * N + i) * N + key]; // Wait, should be query for mask but i for bias? if (pair_mask[(b * N + query) * N + key] == 0.0f) { score = -1e9f; } scores[key] = score; if (score > max_score) max_score = score; } // Softmax float sum_exp = 0.0f; for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) { scores[key] = expf(scores[key] - max_score); sum_exp += scores[key]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) { scores[key] *= inv_sum; } // Compute output float out_reg[16]; for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) out_reg[dd] = 0.0f; for (int key = 0; key < N; key++) { float s = scores[key]; for (int dd = t * d_half; dd < (t + 1) * d_half; dd++) { out_reg[dd] += s * V_s[key * d + dd]; } } // Write output (both threads write all channels) for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) { int out_idx = ((b * N + i) * N + query) * D + head * d + dd; scratch[out_idx] = out_reg[dd]; } } ``` Wait, there's an issue with the shuffle. `__shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, dot, 1)` exchanges between adjacent threads. Thread 0 and thread 1 are for the same query. Thread 2 and thread 3 are for query 1, etc. XOR with 1 swaps between thread pairs: - Thread 0 (binary 000000) XOR 1 = thread 1 (000001) ✓ - Thread 2 (000010) XOR 1 = thread 3 (000011) ✓ - Thread 4 (000100) XOR 1 = thread 5 (000101) ✓ So `__shfl_xor_sync(mask, dot, 1)` correctly pairs threads for the same query! After the shuffle, both threads in the pair have the full dot product. Then both compute the same score, same softmax, same output. There's duplication but no correctness issue. For the write, both threads write the same output. This is a race condition! Both threads are writing to the same memory location. If they write the same value, it's technically fine for common GPU memory models (write same value is safe), but it's wasteful. To avoid the race, I can have only 1 thread per query write. For example, thread with t=0 writes, t=1 skips. Alternatively, each thread can write only its half of the channels. But then the output in scratch needs to be assembled. Hmm. Actually, it's simpler to just let both threads write the full output. In practice, since they write the same value, there's no correctness issue (though it wastes bandwidth). Wait, scratch is in global memory. Having 64 threads but only 32 queries means 2 threads per query. Both threads writing the same address is definitely a waste. Let me fix it. I can use a shared memory buffer for the output. Each thread writes its computed channels to a shared memory buffer, then one thread writes to global memory. But that's more complex. Alternatively, just have 1 thread per query do everything, and the extra thread helps only with dot product reduction. After that, t=1 thread can return early. ```c if (t == 1) return; // after computing and reducing dot product // But then t=1 thread doesn't participate in loading K/V or in __syncthreads() after K/V load. // So I need to make sure t=1 returns AFTER all __syncthreads() calls. ``` Wait, K/V loading has `__syncthreads()`. If t=1 returns before the second `__syncthreads()`, it'll deadlock. CUDA has `__syncthreads_and` but not conditional sync. Actually, `__syncthreads()` requires ALL threads to reach it. So t=1 can't return early. New approach: don't have t=1 thread skip. Instead, have it compute everything but not write to global memory. Or, have only t=0 threads write. ```c if (t == 0) { for (int dd = 0; dd < d; dd++) { int out_idx = ...; scratch[out_idx] = out_reg[dd]; } } ``` But t=1 still needs the `out_reg` values. Actually, out_reg is the same for both threads if they compute everything. So t=0 writes, t=1 skips. For the bias in attention: I used `bias[((b * h + head) * N + i) * N + key]`. Let me check: bias shape should be (B, h, N, N). So bias[b, head, i, key]. My indexing gives base = b*h*N*N + head*N*N + i*N + key. But the preprocess wrote: `bias[((b * h + out_c) * N + i) * N + j]` where out_c is head, i is row, j is column. So bias for row i and key k is: `bias[b, head, i, k]`. Yes, this matches. In attention, I'm reading `bias[b, head, i, key]` where key is the column dimension. Correct. For the mask: it should be `pair_mask[(b * N + query) * N + key]`. Let me fix this.
